201412	3	2014-12-17	meeting	FISCHER	So moved.	5	5
201412	4	2014-12-17	meeting	YELLEN	Without objection, thank you.	5	5
201412	5	2014-12-17	meeting	YELLEN	I’m going to start off by calling on Simon to give us his report on market developments.	61	6
201412	6	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201412	7	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Lorie and I will be splitting the Desk briefing into two parts.	61	6
201412	8	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	I will discuss financial market developments and some issues related to open market operations.	61	6
201412	9	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	After questions and answers on this section, Lorie will discuss testing and some related issues with supplementary normalization tools.	61	6
201412	10	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Over the intermeeting period, US economic data were generally viewed as positive and changes in financial conditions were mixed.	61	6
201412	11	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	International developments were in focus with more-accommodative policy adopted or communicated by major central banks, driven by concerns over low inflation and weak growth.	61	6
201412	12	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Low inflation concerns were amplified by sharp declines in oil prices.	61	6
201412	13	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	These declines weighed on prices of risk assets linked to energy amid a broad reassessment of the possibility for structurally lower oil prices due to supply-side developments as well as ongoing global growth concerns.	61	6
201412	14	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	These factors also contributed to an increase in realized and implied volatility across markets late in the intermeeting period.	61	6
201412	15	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	I thought it would be helpful to look back over the year, as price moves across some major asset classes have been meaningfully different from market expectations at the end of last year.	61	6
201412	16	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	While large errors in forecasting asset prices should be expected, the constellation of forecast errors for 2014, shown in the top-left panel, is consistent with easier monetary policy abroad than previously anticipated, a positive oil supply shock, and a relative outperformance of the US economy.	61	6
201412	17	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The size of the unexpected decline in Treasury yields is particularly striking, given that most believe the Federal Reserve is nearing the start of policy normalization around mid-2015.	61	6
201412	18	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Over the remainder of this exhibit, I will focus on two aspects of domestic markets:  the market-implied path of the target federal funds rate and declines in measures of longer-dated inflation compensation.	61	6
201412	19	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The top-right panel shows the market-implied federal funds rate path.	61	6
201412	20	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Despite better-than-expected US economic data and some Federal Reserve communications that were perceived as less accommodative than anticipated, market-implied rates changed little on net over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201412	21	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Looking back further, the curve has shifted down and flattened substantially since the end of 2013 despite ongoing improvements in domestic labor market conditions and notable upward revisions in Committee participants’ SEP federal funds rate paths.	61	6
201412	22	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This has resulted in a sizable divergence between the market-implied and SEP-projected rate paths.	61	6
201412	23	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This divergence can be seen in the middle-left panel, which shows the spreads between median SEP rate projections as of the September meeting and corresponding market-implied rates.	61	6
201412	24	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	These spreads widened over the course of the year, especially after the September meeting, and are around their widest levels since rate projections were added to the SEP.	61	6
201412	25	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk’s most recent surveys asked respondents to rate the importance of various factors in explaining the difference between the SEP median dots and the market-implied path.	61	6
201412	26	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Dealers and buy-side survey respondents generally assigned the highest importance to different outlooks for the US economy, the view that the SEP median does not represent the Committee’s forecast, and the possibility of remaining at the zero lower bound.	61	6
201412	27	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Negative term premiums and technical factors were assigned a lower importance.	61	6
201412	28	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The middle-right panel uses responses to a range of survey questions, along with the SEP numbers and market-implied rates, to help further identify the importance of these factors.	61	6
201412	29	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The dark blue bars represent the difference between the median SEP dot and the median of the fourth- to seventh-lowest SEP dots, which many market participants believe most closely align with the likely path of the policy rate.	61	6
201412	30	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	These dark blue bars are relatively large, which suggests that a key fact in understanding the spread between the median SEP dot and the market-implied path might be that market participants do not view the median dot as representing the future path of the policy rate.	61	6
201412	31	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The light blue bars represent the difference between the median of the fourth to seventh dots and the median of Desk survey respondents’ modal path for the target rate.	61	6
201412	32	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	These differences are quite small, which may indicate that market participants close to the Desk survey median have a similar view as to the outlook for the economy and its implications for the target rate as the FOMC participants underlying the median of the fourth to seventh dots.	61	6
201412	33	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The dark red bars represent the difference between the mean expectation and the modal expectation for the target rate from the Desk’s surveys, which incorporates the perceived possibility of remaining at or returning to the zero lower bound along with other downside risks.	61	6
201412	34	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In 2016 and 2017, this accounts for around 30 to 35 basis points of the difference between the median SEP dot and the market-implied rate.	61	6
201412	35	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Finally, the light red bars represent the difference between the mean of respondents’ federal funds rate probability distributions and the market-implied rate, a measure of risk premiums in futures rates.	61	6
201412	36	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	These differences are very small at earlier horizons but increase substantially in 2017 to a negative term premium of around 55 basis points.	61	6
201412	37	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In sum, this panel indicates that the spread between the median SEP forecast and the market-implied rate in 2015 and 2016 may be largely due to differences in the perception of the mapping between the SEP dots and the likely path of policy, along with downside risks to modal federal funds rate forecasts.	61	6
201412	38	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	While the more substantial divergence in 2017 might be of less concern because of the uncertainty around three- year-ahead forecasts, it does line up with the continued decline in medium- and long- dated forward rates.	61	6
201412	39	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	One explanation for this decline is an increase in the market price associated with certain types of adverse economic shocks, as described in a memo from the staff at the Minneapolis Fed.	61	6
201412	40	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The fall in forward rates has been more pronounced in nominal than real rates, producing large declines in market-based measures of inflation compensation.	61	6
201412	41	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The Board staff’s measures of 5- and 10-year inflation compensation have declined roughly 25 to 30 basis points over the period.	61	6
201412	42	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Both the 5- and 10-year breakeven rates, at roughly 1.33 percent and 1.62 percent, respectively, are now at their lowest levels since the height of the euro-area crisis in mid-2010.	61	6
201412	43	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition, the Board staff’s measure of five-year, five-year-forward inflation compensation, shown in the bottom-left panel, is at its lowest level since late 2008, when TIPS market liquidity was highly strained.	61	6
201412	44	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	As we noted at the previous meeting, the sharp declines in these market-based inflation measures contrasts with the relative stability of primary dealers’ longer-term inflation expectations, also shown in this panel, and with other survey-based measures.	61	6
201412	45	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	While not all market measures of forward inflation compensation are as low as the one estimated by Board staff, many of them suggest longer-dated inflation risk premiums are negative under the assumption that long forward inflation expectations remain well anchored near the FOMC’s longer-run inflation objective.	61	6
201412	46	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Amid the relative stability of survey-based inflation measures, the Desk surveys again asked respondents to decompose the decline in the market-based five-year, five- year-forward breakeven inflation rate since early September, in an attempt to capture views about sentiments underlying market pricing.	61	6
201412	47	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The bottom-right panel shows that dealer and buy-side respondents, on average, attributed roughly half of the decline to lower expected CPI inflation, while the other half was attributed to changes in the inflation risk premium and other risk premiums.	61	6
201412	48	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This decomposition is similar to that for the October surveys.	61	6
201412	49	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Factors cited in the surveys as contributing to lower market inflation expectations include declines in energy prices, appreciation of the US dollar, and concerns about global growth and inflation.	61	6
201412	50	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	More marginally, some market participants have cited concerns that the Federal Reserve may tighten policy before the economy is sufficiently resilient to withstand higher interest rates.	61	6
201412	51	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Some have also suggested that the deteriorating TIPS market liquidity may be a factor behind the narrowing in breakeven inflation rates.	61	6
201412	52	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	However, Desk analysis suggests there was relatively little change in market functioning over most of the intermeeting period, though poor liquidity may have exacerbated the particularly pronounced drop in breakeven inflation rates late last week.	61	6
201412	53	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The top-left panel of your next exhibit shows that measures of forward inflation compensation declined in other developed economies over the course of this year, though Japanese measures are little changed.	61	6
201412	54	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Forward inflation compensation declines in the United States are larger than in the euro area over the past year, which may reflect greater sensitivity of domestic inflation to declines in oil prices among other factors.	61	6
201412	55	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Low inflation, along with declining inflation expectations and weak economic growth abroad, has motivated a variety of more-accommodative monetary policy actions or communications outside of the United States.	61	6
201412	56	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This has furthered the divergence in the expected policy rate paths between the Federal Reserve and other central banks in developed countries.	61	6
201412	57	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	To encourage inflation and stimulate the economy in Japan, the BOJ unexpectedly announced an expansion of its Quantitative and Qualitative Monetary Easing, or QQE, program to limit deflationary risks, and Prime Minister Abe took steps to postpone the second planned consumption tax increase.	61	6
201412	58	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Both the size and timing of the BOJ announcement surprised market participants, leading to a sharp depreciation of the yen, increase in Japanese equity prices, and decline in 30-year Japanese government bond yields, as shown in the top-right panel.	61	6
201412	59	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The BOJ decision coincided with an announcement from the Government Pension Investment Fund (GPIF) indicating that it would reduce its portfolio allocation to domestic bonds and increase its allocation to equities and international fixed-income assets by more than expected.	61	6
201412	60	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Most market participants expect other Japanese pensions to follow the GPIF strategy, which could result in roughly $90 billion and $175 billion being reallocated toward US Treasury securities and US equities, respectively, over the next year.	61	6
201412	61	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Concerns over low inflation in the euro area also motivated more-accommodative communications from the ECB Governing Council.	61	6
201412	62	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	At its November meeting, the Governing Council explicitly stated its intention to increase the size of the ECB balance sheet toward early 2012 levels, which will require a roughly 1 trillion euro expansion, as shown in the middle-left panel.	61	6
201412	63	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	A subsequent speech from President Draghi bolstered expectations that the ECB will purchase sovereign bonds.	61	6
201412	64	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants continue to believe that the current suite of ECB measures is insufficient to increase the balance sheet toward early 2012 levels or sufficiently raise inflation expectations.	61	6
201412	65	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Indeed, take-up in the ECB’s recently announced targeted longer-term refinancing operations has been toward the lower end of market expectations.	61	6
201412	66	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In response, market participants have increased the probability they attach to a sovereign bond purchase program in the first quarter of next year.	61	6
201412	67	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Over the intermeeting period, front-month crude oil futures prices declined around 30 percent and longer-dated oil futures prices declined 15 to 20 percent.	61	6
201412	68	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Market participants believe that the price declines have been driven primarily by supply dynamics, particularly the recent OPEC decision to maintain current production levels amid faster-than-expected growth in US shale output.	61	6
201412	69	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the middle-right panel, in contrast to the decline in oil prices, industrial metals prices—one potential proxy for global economic activity—have been relatively stable, suggesting that oil price declines are likely only secondarily due to demand dynamics.	61	6
201412	70	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The oil price declines are squeezing the profit margins of many US energy- related firms and contributing to a widening in the high-yield corporate credit spread index, also shown in the middle-right panel, which has a relatively high energy weighting.	61	6
201412	71	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This spread widening may also reflect recent elevated high-yield issuance and a reported increase in credit differentiation as domestic policy normalization approaches.	61	6
201412	72	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The bottom-left panel shows US dollar appreciation against major developed economies and large non-dollarized oil exporting countries over the intermeeting period and year-to-date.	61	6
201412	73	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The US dollar appreciation against major developed economy currencies, shown in solid shades of blue, is largely due to diverging monetary policy expectations and growth outlooks.	61	6
201412	74	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	US dollar appreciation against large petroleum exporting countries, shown in dashed shades of blue, largely reflects the impact of lower oil prices on these local economies.	61	6
201412	75	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The Russian ruble, not shown in the chart due to its extremely large moves, has depreciated significantly, especially over the past couple of days.	61	6
201412	76	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The depreciation reflects a combination of low oil prices and the effect of western sanctions and ongoing tensions in Ukraine.	61	6
201412	77	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This, along with the increased possibility of defaults on the large stock of foreign-currency-denominated corporate debt, is leading to increased financial-stability risk in Russia.	61	6
201412	78	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In response to the recent depreciation, the Russian central bank increased its main policy rate from 10.5 percent to 17 percent on Monday night.	61	6
201412	79	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The recent declines in oil prices and concerns over global growth have weighed on emerging market risk assets, as shown in the bottom-right panel, and declines in EM asset prices accelerated late in the intermeeting period.	61	6
201412	80	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In contrast, there has recently been a meteoric rise in the Shanghai Composite Index, also shown in this panel.	61	6
201412	81	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The Shanghai index has risen nearly 20 percent following an interest rate cut by the PBOC in mid-November.	61	6
201412	82	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This rate cut increased market expectations for further PBOC easing amid the subdued mainland inflation prints and activity data.	61	6
201412	83	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Turning to Desk operations, negative interest rates on eligible euro-area investments continue to present challenges to the management of the SOMA euro foreign reserves portfolio.	61	6
201412	84	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The approach taken to reduce the influence of negative interest rates on the portfolio’s return has resulted in an increase in the duration of the portfolio, as shown in the top-left panel of your next exhibit.	61	6
201412	85	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	As discussed in a memo distributed before the meeting, the Desk would like to make some changes to the euro portfolio investment strategy to better balance the tradeoff between maintaining maturity liquidity and earning interest income in a negative interest rate environment.	61	6
201412	86	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Assuming the rate structure in France and Germany doesn’t move lower, this revised investment strategy would likely allow us to keep the duration of the portfolio below the FOMC-authorized limit of 18 months.	61	6
201412	87	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	However, that 18-month limit will constrain our ability to implement the revised strategy if French and German interest rates fall further.	61	6
201412	88	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	To avoid a situation in which the current duration limit becomes a binding constraint on the investment strategy in the negative interest rate environment, we plan to recommend that the FOMC increase the duration limit from 18 to 24 months.	61	6
201412	89	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk views the reauthorization of the foreign operations at the January 2015 FOMC meeting as an opportune time to make such a change.	61	6
201412	90	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Turning to the top-right panel, with the end of the asset purchase program in October, the SOMA currently stands at just under $4.3 trillion, including about $2.5 trillion in Treasury securities and about $1.8 trillion in MBS.	61	6
201412	91	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Rollovers of Treasury securities and reinvestments of agency principal payments will maintain the par value of the portfolio at its current level until these policies are ended.	61	6
201412	92	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	For context, only about $3 billion in Treasury holdings will mature in 2015, but the staff estimates that principal paydowns on MBS and maturing agency debt will total almost $300 billion next year.	61	6
201412	93	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	MBS reinvestment operations have generally gone smoothly, although in mid-November the Desk cancelled an MBS operation due to a technical glitch.	61	6
201412	94	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The problem was subsequently resolved, and the operation was successfully completed a few days later.	61	6
201412	95	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Additionally, the Desk began trading today with the three small firms that are participating in the Mortgage Operations Counterparty Pilot Program, as part of our ongoing efforts to explore ways to broaden access to open market operations and improve operational capacity and resiliency.	61	6
201412	96	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The Desk continues to take other steps to enhance our operational readiness.	61	6
201412	97	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	We plan to conduct small-value sales of MBS early next year.	61	6
201412	98	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition, with tremendous support from the management and staff at FRB Chicago, the Markets Group has replicated the infrastructure and human capital necessary to ensure the implementation of all operations, market monitoring, and associated analytical work deemed critical should the New York Fed and its staff be affected by a serious contingency event.	61	6
201412	99	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	At the previous meeting I discussed a new service the Federal Reserve could offer known as segregated balance accounts (SBAs).	61	6
201412	100	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The discussion of this service in the October minutes generated considerable interest amongst Fed watchers.	61	6
201412	101	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	As you recall, SBAs are designed to improve competition in the deposit market.	61	6
201412	102	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The mechanism through which they would improve competition is similar to, but different in important respects from, the use of RRPs.	61	6
201412	103	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants interpreted the discussion in the minutes as suggesting SBAs would receive important regulatory relief which would substantially lower the balance sheet costs of attracting funds into such accounts.	61	6
201412	104	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	As you know, such relief was not part of the plan for the service, although some other types of regulatory decisions were required for SBAs to be viable.	61	6
201412	105	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	In addition, various system changes would be required.	61	6
201412	106	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	The staff currently judges that in light of the competing priorities related to normalization and the small marginal effect SBAs would likely have on interest rate control given the readiness of other tools, further work on implementing SBAs should be shelved for now.	61	6
201412	107	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	This judgment is supported by the reaction to a request for feedback on continuing work on SBAs in a memo that Bill English and I sent to the Committee.	61	6
201412	108	2014-12-17	meeting	POTTER	Madam Chair, this completes my part of the Desk briefing.	61	6

201412	273	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201412	274	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	I’ll begin again on exhibit 3 with a summary of the recent testing of the term deposit facility and reverse repos, and a staff proposal to continue ON RRP testing in 2015.	61	6
201412	275	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	These items were discussed in two of the staff memos you received ahead of the meeting.	61	6
201412	276	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Over the intermeeting period, the staff completed a series of eight TDF test operations that included an early withdrawal option.	61	6
201412	277	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Including an early withdrawal option allowed banks to count term deposits as HQLA for purposes of the Liquidity Coverage Ratio.	61	6
201412	278	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The first few operations in the series provided a test of the attractiveness of an early withdrawal option, as the maximum award amount and TDF rate were the same as in previous operations.	61	6
201412	279	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Consistent with the feedback from banks before the series was initiated, allotment amounts in these operations with the option increased notably.	61	6
201412	280	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Subsequent operations in the series, shown in the dark blue line in the middle-left panel, also incorporated a higher maximum award amount and incremental increases in the TDF rate.	61	6
201412	281	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Take-up in these operations rose, on average, by an increase of $35 billion per basis point in the TDF rate.	61	6
201412	282	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	More broadly, the increased participation in these operations relative to those without the early withdrawal option, shown in the light blue line, was driven by both the introduction of the early withdrawal option and the higher maximum award amounts.	61	6
201412	283	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The staff will continue to evaluate the results of the most recent series of operations in developing options for additional TDF testing next year.	61	6
201412	284	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Over the intermeeting period, the Desk proceeded with testing of both term and overnight reverse repos as directed by the Committee.	61	6
201412	285	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The details of these operations are shown in the middle-right panel for your reference.	61	6
201412	286	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	So far, operations have proceeded smoothly.	61	6
201412	287	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The announcement and conduct of term RRP operations to date provide a preliminary indication that term RRPs could be an effective supplementary tool for mitigating the effects of year-end dynamics on money market rates.	61	6
201412	288	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Additionally, recent overnight RRP testing has reinforced the idea that increases in the ON RRP offering rate put upward pressure on both secured and unsecured overnight rates.	61	6
201412	289	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The coming weeks will provide a useful test of how these supplementary tools could work in combination to support interest rate control over year- and quarter-ends.	61	6
201412	290	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	With respect to the term RRP operations, the Desk conducted two of the four announced operations.	61	6
201412	291	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As shown in the bottom-left panel, counterparties placed $102 billion and $75 billion in bids in the first two operations, respectively.	61	6
201412	292	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Participants generally viewed the award rates for the two operations, which were 8 basis points and 7 basis points, respectively, as roughly equivalent to their expectations for ON RRP rates through January 5, plus a small term premium to compensate for locking up liquidity.	61	6
201412	293	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Dealers and money market funds expect the award rate to fall further as operations approach year-end.	61	6
201412	294	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Based on the operations conducted so far, some firms appear to be substituting funds that they would otherwise invest in ON RRP operations into the term operations.	61	6
201412	295	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Looking at the bottom-right panel, prime and government money funds decreased their participation in the ON RRP operations that followed each of the term operations—shown by the red bars—by an amount that was roughly similar to their allotment in the term operations, which are represented by the blue bars.	61	6
201412	296	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As a result of this substitution, the total amount of reverse repos outstanding, including both term and ON RRPs, is currently $135 billion, which is actually a bit lower than the take-up in ON RRPs just before the term operations began.	61	6
201412	297	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	While we will learn more in coming weeks, the term RRP operation results to date, combined with anecdotal reports and the relatively benign expectations for year- end stress implied by market-based measures currently, together suggest that the use of term RRP operations over year-end may moderate some of the volatility in money market rates that we observed around the September quarter-end.	61	6
201412	298	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The Desk also completed the directed series of ON RRP operations that involved modest, pre-announced changes in the offering rate.	61	6
201412	299	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	While several other factors also affected money market rates during the testing period, the tests provided further indication that ON RRPs provide a soft floor for both secured and unsecured money market rates.	61	6
201412	300	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As shown in the top-left panel of your last exhibit, the increases in the ON RRP offering rate to 7 basis points and 10 basis points corresponded with increases in secured and unsecured rates, including the federal funds effective rate.	61	6
201412	301	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The distribution of rates on federal funds trades, shown in the top-right panel, also shifted higher with each increase in the overnight RRP rate.	61	6
201412	302	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	For example, when the overnight RRP rate was 5 basis points, as shown in the dark blue line, less than 1 percent of brokered federal funds trades occurred below the overnight RRP rate.	61	6
201412	303	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As the overnight RRP offering rate was increased to 10 basis points, the distribution shifted higher, and again only about 1 percent of trades took place below the new overnight RRP offering rate.	61	6
201412	304	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Market participants believe that the increase in the federal funds effective rate during the testing period was primarily due to increased bargaining power by FHLBs.	61	6
201412	305	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Moreover, it does not appear as if FHLBs had to change their behavior meaningfully in order to obtain higher rates.	61	6
201412	306	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Participation by FHLBs in overnight RRP operations—the dark blue line in the middle-left panel—was largely unchanged during the testing period.	61	6
201412	307	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	More broadly, federal funds trading volumes—the red line in the same panel—also remained stable.	61	6
201412	308	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Stable federal funds volumes alongside a higher federal funds effective rate could also indicate that foreign banking organizations are still willing to participate in IOER arbitrage at a spread to the IOER rate that is narrower than its level prior to the changes in the overnight RRP rate.	61	6
201412	309	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	This suggests that balance sheet costs may not be the primary factor currently affecting the rate at which FBOs borrow federal funds outside of key financial reporting dates.	61	6
201412	310	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Imperfect competition among banks may also play a role in determining the rates at which they borrow funds in order to conduct IOER arbitrage.	61	6
201412	311	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Secured rates, including the triparty repo rates, shown in the middle-right panel, also shifted up in response to the move in the overnight RRP rate.	61	6
201412	312	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	This rise in rates partially reflected the increased bargaining power of a broader set of cash lenders.	61	6
201412	313	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	However, other factors, such as changes in collateral supply and GSE cash management needs, also reportedly put upward pressure on repo rates, particularly early in the testing period when the overnight RRP rate was 5 basis points.	61	6
201412	314	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	While these changes to the overnight RRP rate have been informative, additional testing of overnight RRPs could further improve our understanding of this supplementary tool.	61	6
201412	315	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As outlined in the bottom-left panel, the staff recommends extending overnight RRP testing through January 2016.	61	6
201412	316	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Doing so would provide continuity in the usage of the tool, particularly given investors’ reported emphasis on developing relationships so as to ensure ongoing access to their counterparties’ balance sheets.	61	6
201412	317	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	If the operations were to cease in January, it is possible that, at the beginning of normalization, frictions in reestablishing these relationships could temporarily hamper the efficacy of an overnight RRP facility.	61	6
201412	318	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Additionally, ending overnight RRP testing could be read by some investors as suggesting that normalization remains a longer way off or that the Committee might be reconsidering the use of overnight RRPs during normalization.	61	6
201412	319	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Further, since we have conducted relatively few operations at rates above 5 basis points, it may be instructive to continue testing the tool at overnight rates between 5 and 10 basis points to learn more about the size and durability of the resulting market effects, or to test at even higher rates to see if similar effects hold.	61	6
201412	320	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	For these reasons, along with the continued benefits of promoting operational readiness, we recommend extending the overnight RRP exercise for another year.	61	6
201412	321	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As discussed in the revised memo sent to the Committee on Friday, we would like to do so at this meeting, since the current resolution expires only two days after the January 2015 FOMC meeting.	61	6
201412	322	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	The draft resolution is in a separate handout titled “Overnight Reverse Repurchase Resolution.”2  Apart from the change in the terminal date, the draft resolution reflects minor wording changes from the resolution adopted by the Committee at its September 2014 meeting, and maintains the 0 to 5 basis point range in the overnight RRP offering rate.	61	6
201412	323	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	If the change in the resolution is approved, the FOMC minutes would inform the public of the extension.	61	6
201412	324	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Additionally, if the change is approved, the staff will recommend some additional tests at upcoming meetings, after we have had a chance to fully assess the results of the current tests.	61	6
201412	325	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Lastly, as discussed at the October meeting, the Desk reopened the overnight RRP counterparty process for firms meeting the existing eligibility requirements, and we wanted to provide a brief update on the results.	61	6
201412	326	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	As shown in the bottom-right panel, 25 eligible firms submitted applications, including 12 money market funds, 7 banks, 5 FHLBs, and Farmer Mac.	61	6
201412	327	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	If the legal, credit, and compliance assessments of all applicants prove satisfactory, the total number of expanded RRP counterparties will increase to 142.	61	6
201412	328	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	This number incorporates the fact that we had one firm withdraw as a counterparty during the intermeeting period.	61	6
201412	329	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	It was a fairly idiosyncratic circumstance that prompted the withdrawal and is unlikely to be an indication of a broader trend.	61	6
201412	330	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	We plan to announce the new set of counterparties in mid-January and begin operating with them shortly thereafter.	61	6
201412	331	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	61	6
201412	332	2014-12-17	meeting	LOGAN	That concludes my prepared remarks.	61	6

201412	399	2014-12-17	meeting	YELLEN	And now we’ll continue with the chart show on the economic and financial situation.	11	1
201412	400	2014-12-17	meeting	YELLEN	Let me turn things over to John Stevens.	11	1
201412	401	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	11	1
201412	402	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	I will be referring to the handout titled “Staff Presentation on the Economic and Financial Situation.”	11	1
201412	403	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The first exhibit summarizes recent information on the labor market.	11	1
201412	404	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The two employment reports that we have received since the October meeting were stronger than we had expected.	11	1
201412	405	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in panel 1, payroll employment gains averaged 278,000 per month over the past three months, 44,000 more per month than we had projected in the October Tealbook.	11	1
201412	406	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	In response, we have marked up our payroll forecast to 250,000 in December and 220,000 per month in the first quarter.	11	1
201412	407	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The unemployment rate, plotted in panel 2, held steady at 5.8 percent in November but has fallen more than 1 percentage point over the past 12 months and, by our estimation, is currently about ½ percentage point above its natural rate.	11	1
201412	408	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	We expect the unemployment rate to edge down in December and to average 5.5 percent in the first quarter.	11	1
201412	409	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The middle panels display two labor market indicators that, in our judgment, point to some recent improvement but also suggest more slack than is indicated by the unemployment rate.	11	1
201412	410	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in panel 3, the labor force participation rate (the black line) is below both the staff’s estimate of its trend (the green line) and the level predicted by a simple regression of the participation rate on the current and lagged values of the output gap (the red line).	11	1
201412	411	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Similarly, panel 4 indicates that the share of workers reporting that they are working part time for economic reasons remains considerably above the level we might expect given the output gap.	11	1
201412	412	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Other labor market indicators also suggest that conditions are continuing to improve.	11	1
201412	413	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown by the black line in panel 5, the rate of job openings in the JOLTS has risen sharply this year.	11	1
201412	414	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Meanwhile, both the hiring rate (the red line) and the quits rate (the blue line) have moved up as well.	11	1
201412	415	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Panel 6 provides results from a recent inquiry conducted by the Reserve Banks about the hiring plans of their business contacts.	11	1
201412	416	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As noted on the first row of the table, the average share of firms planning to increase hiring over the next 12 months moved up to 52 percent, the highest reading since we started asking this question several years ago.	11	1
201412	417	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Among firms planning to increase hiring, more than one-fourth plan to raise starting pay to help with recruiting, a noticeably higher share than in early 2013.	11	1
201412	418	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The reported willingness to raise starting pay may, as shown in the last line of the table, reflect increases in the share of firms reporting difficulties finding workers with the required skills.	11	1
201412	419	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As noted in a box in the Tealbook, “Indicators of Labor Shortages,” other evidence also points to increasing instances of skill shortages, but the extent to which such shortages are restraining hiring and production appears to be about in line with what one would expect given measures of labor market and product market slack.	11	1
201412	420	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The next exhibit summarizes other recent economic developments and provides an update to our near-term GDP forecast based on information received after we closed the Tealbook projection.	11	1
201412	421	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in the inset box in panel 1, the PCE- relevant portion of retail sales for November was stronger than we had expected, and the gains for earlier months were revised up.	11	1
201412	422	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	In addition, households’ consumption of health-care services during the third quarter looks set to be revised up as well.	11	1
201412	423	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	All told, as shown by the blue bars, we now estimate that real PCE rose at an annual rate of 2¾ percent in the third quarter and forecast that it will increase about 4 percent this quarter.	11	1
201412	424	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The projected pickup this quarter reflects the stronger retail sales data, higher-than-expected motor vehicle sales, and a rebound in energy services spending, which had been held down in the third quarter by unusually cool summer weather.	11	1
201412	425	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	We continue to expect real PCE to increase about 3½ percent in the first quarter, supported by ongoing improvements in employment, household wealth, and consumer sentiment, as well as by the boost to real incomes from the drop in energy prices.	11	1
201412	426	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	In the housing market, panel 2, this morning’s data on single-family starts (the red lines) edged down, and adjusted permits (the black lines) were about unchanged.	11	1
201412	427	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	On net, the data we have received over the past few months have continued to disappoint and are consistent with only gradual increases in housing construction over the near term.	11	1
201412	428	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Nonetheless, we remain steadfast in our assessment that the current low level of construction activity will not be sustained as the strengthening job market and diminishing concerns about the economic outlook eventually fuel a recovery in household formation that will boost the demand for new housing.	11	1
201412	429	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in panel 3, shipments of nondefense capital goods (the black line) were little changed, on net, over the past three months, while orders (the red line) declined.	11	1
201412	430	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	However, with orders running above shipments, we continue to expect investment in equipment and intangibles to rise modestly in the near term.	11	1
201412	431	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in the inset box in panel 4, mining output was little changed in November and is projected to increase at an average annual rate of only 2½ percent in the fourth and first quarters after having risen at a 13 percent pace during the preceding three quarters.	11	1
201412	432	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	This sharp deceleration reflects slower gains in oil and gas extraction and a projected decline in drilling activity.	11	1
201412	433	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Manufacturing production jumped more than 1 percent in November, which was stronger than we had been anticipating, and we now expect manufacturing output to increase at a robust average pace of more than 5 percent per quarter in the fourth and first quarters.	11	1
201412	434	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	These projected gains are consistent both with a step-up in automakers’ assembly schedules in the first quarter (not shown) and with the new orders diffusion indexes plotted in panel 5.	11	1
201412	435	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Although the range of readings on new orders in November was unusually wide, all of the indexes were in positive territory, and the central tendency of these indexes points to solid increases in production over the next few months.	11	1
201412	436	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As indicated on line 1 of panel 6, after folding in the recent information for PCE received following the close of the Tealbook projection, we now estimate that real GDP increased at an annual rate of 4½ percent in the third quarter and that growth will step down to an average pace of 2½ percent this quarter and next.	11	1
201412	437	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Relative to the December Tealbook, line 2, this forecast is about ½ percentage point higher in the third and fourth quarters but little revised in the first quarter.	11	1
201412	438	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The step-down in growth this quarter continues to largely reflect a swing in the contributions from net exports and from federal defense spending.	11	1
201412	439	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Excluding these volatile spending categories, the rate of increase in private domestic final purchases (PDFP), line 4, averages about 3½ percent throughout the near term and does not show the slowdown from the third quarter that we see for real GDP.	11	1
201412	440	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Exhibit 3 describes our medium-term forecast and, for a longer perspective, reviews how that forecast has changed from one year ago.	11	1
201412	441	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in panel 1, our current Tealbook projection is for real GDP growth (the black line) to average 2½ percent per year from 2015 to 2017.	11	1
201412	442	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Compared with our forecast from this time last year (the red dashed line), this projection is about ¾ percentage point weaker per year on average.	11	1
201412	443	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	At the same time, as shown in panel 2, the unemployment rate fell more rapidly over the past year than we had projected in December 2013, and we have marked down the projected path.	11	1
201412	444	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	This change, in an environment of still relatively lackluster GDP growth, led us to mark down our estimates of potential output growth.	11	1
201412	445	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Indeed, as shown in panel 3, the growth of potential in the current Tealbook (the red bars) is projected to be noticeably slower than in last December’s Tealbook (the gray bars).	11	1
201412	446	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Accordingly, the path for the real GDP gap (panel 4) is narrower than it was a year ago, and we currently expect it to close in mid-2016, a little later than the unemployment rate gap, reflecting the additional cyclical slack that we judge to be present in the labor force participation rate.	11	1
201412	447	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As shown in panel 5, an important negative influence on the GDP projection relative to last December has been the sharply higher path over the past year for the real exchange value of the dollar.	11	1
201412	448	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	In addition, a weaker outlook for foreign growth (not shown) also holds down our projection, while oil prices and equity prices have become more supportive.	11	1
201412	449	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	All told, with an unchanged projection for inflation (which I will discuss in a moment) and a little tighter resource utilization, one might expect the path for the federal funds rate—shown in panel 6—to be higher than it was a year ago.	11	1
201412	450	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	However, we also made a small reduction in our estimate of the equilibrium real interest rate, which left our projection for the federal funds rate essentially unrevised.	11	1

201412	460	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Recent readings on inflation compensation and expectations, panel 3, have been mixed.	11	1

201412	462	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	TIPS-based inflation compensation 5-to-10 years ahead (the red line) has declined noticeably, although staff models suggest that much of the decline likely reflected changes in term and liquidity premiums.	11	1
201412	463	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The black lines in panels 4 and 5 give our medium-term projections for total and core PCE price inflation, and the red dashed lines show our projections last December.	11	1

201412	465	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	All told, we expect core PCE inflation of 1.5 percent in 2015, 1.6 percent in 2016, and 1.8 percent in 2017.	11	1
201412	466	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	As energy prices stabilize, total PCE inflation moves back closer to core inflation in the second quarter of 2015.	11	1
201412	467	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Panel 6 summarizes recent indicators of labor compensation.	11	1
201412	468	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	These indicators have been mixed recently but generally continue to point to gains over the past year in the vicinity of 2 percent.	11	1
201412	469	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	The employment cost index—the black line—last quarter posted its largest four-quarter change in several years, but the volatile measure of hourly compensation in the business sector—the blue line—was weaker than we had expected last quarter.	11	1

201412	471	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	On balance, we view the recent gains in compensation as modest, and we continue to expect a gradual acceleration in labor compensation over the next few years as the labor market tightens further.	11	1
201412	472	2014-12-17	meeting	STEVENS	Chris Erceg will now continue our presentation.	11	1
201412	473	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	As indicated in line 1 of the table in exhibit 5, foreign GDP growth appears to have been quite subdued in the second half of the year and well below what we were projecting at the time of your last chart show in June.	11	1
201412	474	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	We expect that foreign growth will pick up next year and remain just above 3 percent over the forecast period.	11	1
201412	475	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	This outlook is little changed from our projection six months ago.	11	1
201412	476	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Although the underlying momentum of the foreign economy is weaker, several factors—in part engendered by this weakness—should boost growth in the period ahead:  greater monetary policy stimulus, substantial currency depreciation—panel 2—and the dramatic fall in oil prices since midsummer.	11	1
201412	477	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	These factors also have important consequences for the US economy, as I will examine later in my briefing.	11	1
201412	478	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	A good deal of the current weakness and forecast uncertainty is centered in the euro area and Japan.	11	1
201412	479	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Euro-area GDP, line 3, is currently rising at a less than 1 percent pace, consistent with the declines in PMIs shown in panel 3, and we have growth picking up only gradually.	11	1
201412	480	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Monetary policy stimulus measures announced this year—including the targeted longer-term refinancing operations, or TLTRO, in June and the September commitment to employ asset purchases to expand the balance sheet to 2012 levels—have helped push down bond yields and the euro.	11	1
201412	481	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Given the usual lags in monetary transmission, we project that the effects of these measures will become more apparent next year:  We estimate that they will boost euro-area GDP growth by nearly ½ percentage point on average in 2015 and 2016, when the stimulus from a lower euro is factored in.	11	1
201412	482	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	This estimate assumes that the ECB will announce additional asset purchases in January amounting to 200 billion euros.	11	1
201412	483	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The ECB is currently considering more-aggressive measures, but we have not yet built these into our baseline outlook.	11	1
201412	484	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	In addition to the stimulus from monetary easing and a weaker currency, we also see euro-area GDP as getting a significant boost from lower oil prices and waning fiscal and financial headwinds.	11	1
201412	485	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Japan, line 4, will also be happy to celebrate the end of 2014.	11	1
201412	486	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Although we expect Japan to move out of recession in the fourth quarter, GDP is likely to have flatlined in the second half of the year.	11	1
201412	487	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Due to the potent effect of the April VAT tax hike on domestic demand—consumption, panel 5, remains below its level prior to the start of “Abenomics”—the second installment of the tax hike was deferred until the spring of 2017.	11	1
201412	488	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Amid growing concern that the weak economy was undermining progress in boosting inflation to 2 percent, the BOJ decided in late October to step up its pace of JGB purchases under its Quantitative and Qualitative Monetary Easing Program, QQE—to about 16 percent of GDP at an annual rate, panel 6—and the government pension fund simultaneously announced a large shift in its portfolio holdings toward domestic and foreign equity.	11	1
201412	489	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	We see GDP growth picking up next year as the lower yen boosts exports and as increased monetary accommodation feeds through to domestic demand.	11	1
201412	490	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Turning to your next exhibit, real GDP growth in the EMEs in the third and fourth quarters looks to be well below what we projected in June.	11	1
201412	491	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The weakness is concentrated in Latin America.	11	1
201412	492	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	GDP growth in Mexico, line 2, was a dismal 2 percent in the third quarter, although most recent indicators—including manufacturing PMIs, panel 2—suggest greater underlying strength, and we see growth recovering to nearly 3½ percent next year.	11	1
201412	493	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	By contrast, we are much less optimistic about Brazil.	11	1
201412	494	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Brazil’s overly expansive policies following the global financial crisis have led to persistently high inflation, panel 3.	11	1
201412	495	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	With monetary and fiscal policy likely to be tight, Brazil’s growth prospects will depend on its ability to implement structural reforms and rebuild confidence.	11	1
201412	496	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Skipping halfway across the globe, real GDP growth in China, line 4 of the table, appears to have moderated a bit from its robust third-quarter pace.	11	1
201412	497	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Falling property prices this year, panel 4, have contributed to some slowing in residential investment from its previous very rapid pace, and recent indicators suggest some deceleration in manufacturing.	11	1
201412	498	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Even so, China’s slowdown appears to be in line with what we have been expecting for some time, and retail sales, panel 5, remain reasonably strong.	11	1
201412	499	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	We expect that policy stimulus—including increased lending to the banking sector by the PBOC as well as steps to support the property sector—will allow GDP growth to continue to top 7 percent.	11	1
201412	500	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Of course, there are many downside risks, especially given the weakness in property markets.	11	1
201412	501	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	While I have already indicated that we see lower oil prices as likely to boost growth in oil-importing economies, your next exhibit provides a closer look at how the large fall in oil prices since June, panel 1, has affected the outlook.	11	1
201412	502	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	As best as we can tell, most of the decline reflects favorable supply surprises, including to US and Libyan oil production, and from OPEC’s decision to not curtail production.	11	1
201412	503	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Even so, slow growth in the global economy has also played an important role.	11	1
201412	504	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	A fall in the price of oil affects net oil importers, such as the United States, through several channels (panel 2).	11	1
201412	505	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	First, by reducing transfers to oil exporters, a lower oil price effectively acts as a tax cut to oil-importing countries.	11	1
201412	506	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Second, lower oil prices raise potential output both through boosting oil usage in production and through capital deepening.	11	1
201412	507	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Of course, for major energy producers such as the United States, this effect will be partly offset by lower oil production.	11	1
201412	508	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Third, lower oil prices reduce inflation.	11	1
201412	509	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Finally, the output response depends on monetary policy.	11	1
201412	510	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Although oil-importing economies should benefit from declining oil prices, observers have raised concerns that these declines may exacerbate disinflationary pressures and potentially even harm the economy.	11	1
201412	511	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Such a risk seems particularly relevant for the euro area and Japan.	11	1
201412	512	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	To explore this possibility, we used the staff’s general-equilibrium, perfect-foresight SIGMA model to gauge the effect of a 20 percent permanent decline in the oil price—caused by a favorable supply shock— from its current level.	11	1
201412	513	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The responses we will report later for the United States differ somewhat from those of the staff’s judgmental assessment, which is based on the FRB/US model.	11	1
201412	514	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The middle panels show the response of euro-area GDP growth, headline inflation, and the policy rate, while the bottom panels show the corresponding variables for the United States.	11	1
201412	515	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	A key difference between the two economies is that our baseline assumes that liftoff of the policy rate occurs in the second quarter of 2015 in the United States, but not until early 2018 in the euro area.	11	1
201412	516	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	If inflation expectations are anchored, as we assume in our baseline projections, the shock has fairly similar effects in the euro area and the United States.	11	1
201412	517	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Real GDP growth in this scenario—the red dashed lines in panels 3a and 4a—rises persistently above baseline—the solid black lines.	11	1
201412	518	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Total inflation, panels 3b and 4b, falls because of the relatively direct effect of lower oil prices and because core inflation falls a little as the lower oil prices reduce cost pressures.	11	1
201412	519	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Because inflation and output move in opposite directions, monetary policy is little affected in either economy.	11	1
201412	520	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	We next consider the effects of the same 20 percent decline in oil prices in a scenario in which inflation expectations are not fully anchored and decline as actual inflation falls.	11	1
201412	521	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The decline in inflation expectations has very adverse effects on the euro area.	11	1
201412	522	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Given that the zero bound is expected to bind for a very long time even in the baseline, real interest rates rise substantially and cause euro-area GDP growth to fall below baseline, the green line in panel 3a.	11	1
201412	523	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	By contrast, US GDP in this case— the green line in panel 4a—is very similar to when inflation expectations are anchored, reflecting that monetary policy, panel 4c, cushions the shock by simply lifting off more slowly.	11	1
201412	524	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	To sum up, the fall in oil prices this year is likely to be a net positive for the global economy.	11	1
201412	525	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	However, for economies in which disinflation has been acute and the stability of inflation expectations is uncertain, lower oil prices pose some special challenges.	11	1
201412	526	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Your next exhibit considers how the dollar’s sharp appreciation since June is expected to affect the US economy.	11	1
201412	527	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Our forecast for the path of the dollar over the next two years, panel 1, is about 7 percent higher than we projected in June.	11	1
201412	528	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	On the one hand, we expect the dollar to appreciate a bit further against the AFE currencies, panel 2, as markets are surprised by a modestly faster removal of US policy accommodation than they seem to envision.	11	1
201412	529	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	On the other hand, we continue to project that the dollar will depreciate against the EME currencies, panel 3, as the Asian economies, especially China, rebalance toward domestic demand.	11	1
201412	530	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The stronger path for the dollar and modest weakening of the outlook for foreign growth are likely to translate into much weaker US export growth.	11	1
201412	531	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	We now expect export growth, the blue bars in panel 4, to average only about 2 to 3 percent in 2015 and 2016, about half the pace we projected in June (the red bars).	11	1
201412	532	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Our forecast for import growth is only a tad higher than in June, as the effects of the stronger dollar are partly counterbalanced by downward revisions to the US outlook.	11	1
201412	533	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	All told, as shown in panel 6, we estimate that real net exports will make a contribution to US GDP growth of about minus 0.4 percentage point in both 2015 and 2016, in sharp contrast to the roughly neutral contribution we had expected in June.	11	1
201412	534	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Most of the revision in the net export contribution is due to the stronger dollar.	11	1
201412	535	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Your next exhibit examines the external sector of the US economy during three previous episodes of monetary tightening—those beginning in 1994, 1999, and 2004 —and compares them with our forecast for 2015.	11	1
201412	536	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The dashed vertical lines show the quarter in which a monetary policy tightening sequence began, while the shaded blue regions extend one year before and after this date.	11	1
201412	537	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Two interesting features are apparent from these episodes.	11	1
201412	538	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	First, the broad real dollar, panel 1, shows no tendency to rise during or shortly before past monetary tightenings and, in fact, depreciated substantially around the 2004 episode.	11	1
201412	539	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Second, US exports, panel 2, also grew robustly during each of these tightening episodes.	11	1
201412	540	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Thus, the dollar’s recent sharp appreciation appears a bit unusual and helps account for our forecast of relatively weak export growth in this current episode—the far-right shaded bar in panel 2.	11	1
201412	541	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Even so, the positive gap between foreign and US GDP growth over the forecast period, panel 3, provides some comfort for our forecast that the dollar will not appreciate a good deal more.	11	1
201412	542	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Moreover, the relatively modest gap between expected US and foreign policy rates—panel 4—at least seems consistent with limited upward pressure on the dollar.	11	1
201412	543	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	All told, we do not see our projection for the dollar and US external performance as being markedly at variance with the record of past Fed tightening.	11	1
201412	544	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Export growth is likely to be noticeably weaker but still positive.	11	1
201412	545	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Nonetheless, turning to exhibit 6, overall foreign growth is considerably weaker than in past monetary tightening, and there are serious downside risks in Europe, China, and many other EMEs that—if they materialize—could weigh heavily on the dollar and the US external sector.	11	1
201412	546	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	To judge the impact if these risks materialize and, thus, to conclude this briefing on a perky note, I revisit the SIGMA simulation that was featured in the December Tealbook.	11	1
201412	547	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The scenario assumes that total foreign GDP growth weakens by 1 percentage point over the forecast period per year, and that the broad dollar appreciates 10 percent relative to its baseline path.	11	1
201412	548	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Our scenario suggests that such a weakening would push US GDP growth well below 2 percent next year and core inflation below 1 percent.	11	1
201412	549	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	I will now turn it over to Beth.	11	1

201412	662	2014-12-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I am puzzling over this, and I can’t quite figure it out.	12	1
201412	663	2014-12-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you go to the US case, number 4, “Effects of a 20% Fall in Oil Prices on US Economy in SIGMA,” it seems in the unanchored case that inflation runs noticeably below what happens in the anchored case, which is what you’d expect, but it doesn’t seem to have material growth consequences.	12	1
201412	664	2014-12-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I would have thought that in this “perfect foresight” model, that would affect the real interest rate, and, therefore, have an effect on growth.	12	1
201412	665	2014-12-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But obviously I’m wrong, so—	12	1
201412	666	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	I think it’s helpful to think about it abstracting from the zero bound.	12	1
201412	667	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	If an economy faces an unwanted fall in long-run inflation expectations, then the central bank would take aggressive actions, lowering real interest rates, to undo that.	12	1
201412	668	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	And, at least in the prism of our models, that would actually be stimulative.	12	1
201412	669	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	So you lower long-term interest rates across the maturity spectrum for some time, and that would be stimulative.	12	1
201412	670	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	It is kind of the flip side of what happened in the 1970s, when long-term inflation expectations became unanchored.	12	1
201412	671	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Then we eventually had to raise real interest rates and cause the economy to contract.	12	1
201412	672	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	So what happens with the zero bound is, it matters crucially how long the constraint lasts and how big the shock is.	12	1
201412	673	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Those are two key factors.	12	1
201412	674	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	The shock we’re considering is pretty modest in size, but big enough that you can see it.	12	1
201412	675	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	I think long-term inflation expectations come down ¼ percentage point, and that’s more than what you would think would occur in response to a fall in oil prices, most likely.	12	1
201412	676	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	But still, we wanted it to show up.	12	1
201412	677	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	But the key thing is that, in the US case, with the zero-bound constraint and relatively small shocks, you get lower inflation in the very short term, so that raises the very near-term real interest rate.	12	1
201412	678	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	But then you lower the whole path of interest rates thereafter because you raise rates more gradually, and that lowers real long rates going out for quite a long time.	12	1
201412	679	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	And so it becomes balanced—practically zero in our simulation.	12	1
201412	680	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	Conversely, in the ECB’s case, they’re constrained for a long time, real interest rates go up for several years, and then the economy really gets walloped.	12	1
201412	681	2014-12-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	All right.	12	1
201412	682	2014-12-17	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thanks a lot.	12	1

201412	684	2014-12-17	meeting	ERCEG	That’s right.	12	1

201412	787	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201412	788	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	For this meeting, I have again written down a somewhat restrained projection with real GDP growth of around 2¼ percent for the next couple of years.	13	1
201412	789	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	That rate is in line with the average rate we have seen over the past several years since the recession.	13	1
201412	790	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	We have seen short spurts of stronger growth from time to time, like the one we are seeing this year, but these have been followed by periods of slower growth that bring down the medium-term average growth rate.	13	1
201412	791	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	That pattern has left me generally very hesitant to project that a surge in growth that we see is going to be sustained in general.	13	1
201412	792	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Indeed, over the course of the past year or so, the magnitude of the acceleration in growth we’ve seen has been tempered in both the Tealbook and the SEP.	13	1
201412	793	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The last few months of data are starting to shift my thinking significantly, however.	13	1
201412	794	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Some recent developments that were largely absent during previous growth spurts have led me to believe that the probability of a sustained increase in growth has risen.	13	1
201412	795	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	At the previous meeting, we recognized the substantial improvements that we had seen in labor markets, and the staff described those nicely this afternoon.	13	1
201412	796	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The most recent numbers show continued improvement.	13	1
201412	797	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The November employment report brought average monthly payroll employment gains over the past 12 months to 228,000, a notable increase over the pace shown in the previous years.	13	1
201412	798	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The behavior in the JOLTS data this year, I think, is particularly striking.	13	1
201412	799	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	We’ve seen a substantial increase in job openings this year, a 20 percent gain year-over-year, and hiring has increased significantly as well.	13	1
201412	800	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Moreover, quits are up 20 percent year-over-year, suggesting that workers are becoming more confident in their job prospects.	13	1
201412	801	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The fact that these flow indicators seem to have broken away from the path that has characterized their behavior since the recession is significant, I think.	13	1
201412	802	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	At various points in this expansion, some of us—Governor Tarullo, for example—have noted that measures of labor market fluidity or dynamism appear to be depressed relative to historical standards.	13	1
201412	803	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	In that context, the JOLTS data may be signaling something more fundamental by way of improvement in activity ahead.	13	1
201412	804	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The continuing improvement in labor market conditions has also been reflected in the unemployment rate, which has declined by 1.2 percentage points over the past year, faster than many of us expected and faster than in previous years.	13	1
201412	805	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	This decline has been accompanied by substantial changes in labor force participation and an unusually high rate of workers reporting that they are working part time for economic reasons, two factors the staff singled out in their presentation.	13	1
201412	806	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Given these conditions, there has been an ongoing debate about whether the narrowly defined unemployment rate U-3 continues to be an accurate measure of labor resource utilization.	13	1
201412	807	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	For example, relative to broader measures of unemployment, such as U-6, the current U-3 appears to be significantly below the rate that would be expected based on the relationship between U-3 and U-6 prior to 2007.	13	1
201412	808	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	At past meetings, I have reported on the work of Richmond Fed economists on other measures of labor underutilization that are both broader and take a more differentiated view of the labor market and labor market attachment than does U-6.	13	1
201412	809	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	These so-called nonemployment indexes weight individuals by their relative propensities to flow into employment.	13	1
201412	810	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	In contrast, measures like U-3 or U-6 give everyone without a full-time job a weight of either zero or one.	13	1
201412	811	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	It turns out that, based on the relationship between these nonemployment indexes and U-3 prior to 2007, U-3 is now right in line with what we would expect.	13	1
201412	812	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	So this analysis says that the signal we are getting from the standard unemployment rate neither understates nor overstates the improvement in labor markets.	13	1
201412	813	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	One recurring motif in our discussions about labor market conditions this year has been the idea that the labor market can’t be back to normal if we haven’t yet seen a significant acceleration in wage rates.	13	1
201412	814	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	I’m not sure whether this is a reasonable hypothesis or not, but I do know that wages and unemployment are not tightly correlated in the data.	13	1
201412	815	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	And, moreover, wage acceleration typically lags rather than leads price acceleration.	13	1
201412	816	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	So the lack of apparent wage acceleration—apart from the last couple of quarters for the ECI—does not, in my view, provide much information regarding the degree of slack in the labor market.	13	1
201412	817	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	A strengthening labor market could help bring about a more persistent increase in consumption, and consumption growth in particular.	13	1
201412	818	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	In the past, I have been doubtful about prospects for a sustained increase in consumer spending growth because I felt we lacked evidence of more optimistic household expectations regarding job market prospects that I thought were fundamental to an acceleration in spending growth.	13	1
201412	819	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	But I think that evidence is starting to emerge.	13	1
201412	820	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Both of the consumer confidence indexes have registered significant gains in recent months.	13	1
201412	821	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Drilling down into the Michigan survey, readings on current economic conditions, expected economic conditions 12 months ahead, and expected income growth have all shown sizable gains in the last few months.	13	1
201412	822	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	One should generally be skeptical about blips in these consumer survey measures because they typically don’t improve consumer spending forecasts by much, conditioning on other variables.	13	1
201412	823	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	But these recent moves are striking, and they are well aligned with what is going on in labor market data.	13	1
201412	824	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	So I take them as confirming signals that a meaningful pickup in the consumption growth trend could be ahead.	13	1
201412	825	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	A third area that suggests upside potential, to me, is manufacturing.	13	1
201412	826	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The ISM and most of the regional diffusion indexes have been elevated in recent months.	13	1
201412	827	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Manufacturing employment growth has been significantly more rapid this year than last.	13	1
201412	828	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Monday’s industrial production report showed especially robust growth in manufacturing production in November, and it was across a broad array of industries as well.	13	1
201412	829	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	And investment in equipment and intangibles was strong in the past two quarters.	13	1
201412	830	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Last month we heard an interesting report from a longtime contact of ours at the Association for Manufacturing Technology.	13	1
201412	831	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	He said that the association’s annual trade show this fall saw record attendance, which resulted in double the usual volume of orders.	13	1
201412	832	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	He described the growth in orders as driven by some dramatic improvements in technology that were on display at the show.	13	1
201412	833	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	He cited equipment that incorporates multiple functions—additive, subtractive, and bending—that used to require separate machines to be performed.	13	1
201412	834	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	His association is forecasting continued growth in orders, on the order of 5 to 10 percent, attributable to strength in automotive, aerospace, and industrial sectors.	13	1
201412	835	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Taken together, these developments suggest to me that this growth spurt is more likely to be sustained than previous growth spurts.	13	1
201412	836	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Turning now to inflation, the persistence of inflation around 1½ percent has had people worried about our 2 percent inflation goal.	13	1
201412	837	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	The huge relative price shock from the decline in oil prices and the dollar’s appreciation are going to make it difficult to discern changes in longer- term inflation trends from incoming data on inflation over the next several months.	13	1
201412	838	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	However, the decline in gasoline prices can’t go on forever.	13	1
201412	839	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	What we’re facing in the coming months is just a transitory dip in headline inflation.	13	1
201412	840	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	We can look through that to the core indexes to assess inflation trends, but big energy price moves tend to bleed through to core indexes to some extent as well, with a lag of a couple of months.	13	1
201412	841	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	So the core indexes might be cloudy, too.	13	1
201412	842	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Thus, I’d avoid putting too much weight on incoming inflation data for the next few months.	13	1
201412	843	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	But I don’t think that means policy should be on hold until energy price changes settle down.	13	1
201412	844	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Survey measures of inflation expectations have been stable.	13	1
201412	845	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	TIPS spreads have declined but are still near levels associated with the last several years of trading.	13	1
201412	846	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	So I still believe we have good reason to be reasonably confident that inflation will trend back toward our 2 percent goal.	13	1
201412	847	2014-12-17	meeting	LACKER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
